By MARK HELM c.2003 Hearst Newspapers WASHINGTON _ The U.S. Supreme Court finished its 2002-2003 term last week with two blockbuster rulings on affirmative action and gay rights, but the most mm`mrtant decisions of the session didn't involve cases. Instead, Chief Justice William Rehnquist, 78, and Justice Sandra Day O'Connor, 73 _ the focus of retirement speculation _ decided not to announce their retirement plans. ``Talk of retirement dominated the court this session, making the cases seem almost a side dish to the bigger issue of who would step down, who Bush would choose to nominate and how vicious the confirmation battle would be,'' says Thomas C. Goldstein, a lawyer in private practice who specializes in the high court. Nonetheless, the justices handed down several landmark cases that blurred their image as strong social conservatives. The justices' decisions in a number of cases involving controversial topics were moderate and, at times, liberal. ``On social issues that really divide the country, this court simply isn't as conservative as people try to make it ?::71290 90;42#0997;01ourt specialist at Emory University in Atlanta. In a pair of cases involving the University of Michigan, the court ruled that universities could use racial preferences in choosing students 0a ducation institutions. In the court's first statement on campus affirmative action in a quarter-century, the justices voted 5-4 to uphold the University of Michigan's system of preferences for minorities who apply to its law school. But, by a 6-3 vote, they struck down a point system used by Michigan's undergraduate program, saying it was too formulaic in giving a boost to minorities. The court said schools cannot blindly give minorities extra points just because of their race and instead must take the time to assess each applicant's background and potential. In another closely watched case involving gay rights, the Supreme Court struck down the Texas sodomy law, ruling that the government has no right to control the private lives of homosexuals. The 6-3 decision overturned a court ruling 17 years ago that states could punish gays and lesbians for what such laws historically called deviant sex. The court's decision infuriated conservatives. ``Both the affirmative action and the gay rights decision reflect a political approach to the law that we deplore,'' Jay A. Sekulow, the legal director of the American Center for Law and Justice, a conservative legal advocacy group, said following the decisions. Other decisions also showed a court concerned with tolerance and inclusion. In a 6-3 decision, the court upheld a Virginia law that outlawed cross burning when done to intimidate or threaten. In another 6-3 decision, the court said that state workers could sue their employers when denied time off to care for a sick relative under the federal Family and Medical Leave Act. Writing for the majority, Rehnquist _ one of the court's most conservative members _ praised the law for attacking the stereotype that only women are responsible for family care. But while the court was softening its conservative stance on social issues, the justices were unrelenting in burnishing their reputations for being tough on crime. In three rulings, the court: _ Upheld t`ewo-called Megan's laws, which permits posting identities of sex offenders on the Internet; _ Permitted 25-years-to-life sentences under the ``three-strikes'' laws for relatively minor offenses; _ Allowed cities to put the streets and sidewalks in and around a crime-ridden public housing project off-limits to nonresidents to control crime by controlling access. The term also reaffirmed the importance of O'Connor, a moderately conservative justice whose vote often determines the outcome of a decision. O'Connor was in the majority on each of the court's 15 rulings that were decided by 5-4 votes. During the term, the court issued 76 opinions. ``O'Connor remains the most important member of the court, which basically makes her the most important woman in the government,'' says Ronald Klain, former assistantHcounsel to President Bill Clinton and a former law clerk to Justice Byron White. --  (STORY COULD END HERE. OPTIONAL MATERIAL FOLLOWS) --  In other important decisions, the court ruled that: _ Public libraries that receive federal funds may be forced to install software filters on their computers to shield children from pornography; _ Daniel Stephenson, a Vietnam veteran diagnosed witH sancer decades after being exposed to Agent Orange, can sue the chemical's manufacturers despite a 1984 global class-action settlement designed to end any legal battles over the health effects of the chemical defoliant; _ States can negotiate with pharmaceutical compani's to lower the price of medications in an attempt to hold down the prescription costs; _ Kentucky can force HMOs to open their networks to any health-care provider who wishes to join, allowing patients to see the doctors of their choice _ as long as the physician agrees to the HMO's requirements; _ Medical clinics are not qse clinics; _ Congress can extend the life of copyrights until 70 years after the author's death, up from 50 years. -- (Mark Helm can be reached at 202-263-6425 or at the e-mail address mark(at)hearstdc.com) </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20030627.0061 type=advis > <HEADLINE> ROAD MAP STARTING POINT </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> 06-27-03 21:13:00EDT (BC-EDITORIAL-MIDEAST-BOS) </DATELINE> <TEXT> Fragile as they may be, two negotiated agreements reached among Palestinians and Israelis in recent days offer promise of a turning point for both peoples: initial steps along the road map to peaceful coexistence.  As suggested by the complex maneuvering that produced Palestinian acceptance of a truce and agreement on an Israeli pullback from Gaza and the West Bank town of Bethlehem, President Bush and his advisers will have to be unrelenting and evenhanded in prodding both sides to meet their obligations under the road map. The Americans will also have to be supple and shrewd.  The accord among Palestinian factions to observe a renewable cease-fire for at least three months does not strictly fulfill the terms of the road map. Bush left no doubt about what was required Wednesday when he said: ``The true test for Hamas and terrorist organizations is the complete dismantlement of their terrorist networks, their capacity to blow up the peace process.''  But the Palestinian prime minister, Mahmoud Abbas _ whom Israeli officials want to trust _ argues that any immediate effort by his Palestinian Authority to disarm and arrest commanders of Hamas, Islamic Jihad, and other militias would provoke the equivalent of a civil war. Abbas has warned that he does not now have the security forces or the popular support to take on Hamas and the others. The result of a crackdown, he argues, would be to postpone the cessation of violence necessary to begin implementing the road map.  The case Abbas has made to Hamas and the other militias is that if they accept the truce, at worst they will return the ball back in Israeli Prime Minister Ariel Sharon's court, showing the Americans that it is Sharon, not the Palestinians, who stands in the way of the peace that Washington is trying to foster. Beyond this logic, Hamas was swayed by the threat of a cutoff of funding from Saudi Arabia, diplomatic nudges from Egypt, and the participation of Hamas leaders based in Syria who appeq{0toole played by Marwan Barghouti, the popular leader of the Tanzim militia linked to Yasser Arafat's Fatah faction. From an Israeli prison, Barghouti was allowed to draw up the truce terms Hamas accepted. His central role demonstrated the fragmentation of political authority in the Palestinian camp. The fact that the Israelis let him confer with go-betweens to compose the truce suggests that despite their protestations, they accept Abbas's brief: that a Palestinian cease-fire and an Israeli pullback may be the soundest way to stop terrorism and start implementing the road map.  Bush should pocket the truce and the pullback but keep pushing both sides to do more. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20030627.0062 type=story > <DATELINE> 06-27-03 21:27:00EDT (AM-ADD-NYT-BUDGET-EXCLUS) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200306.gz"

UKRAINE-ELECT (Kiev, Ukraine) -- In defiance of Western leaders and growing popular unrest on the streets, the government of Leonid D. Kuchma, the outgoing president of Ukraine, on Wednesday ignored reports of widespread electoral fraud and certified his prime minister as president-elect. The action ended two days of indecisiveness at the top of government here as a political crisis had deepened, and signaled a decision by Ukraine's incumbent leadership to risk a chill in relations with the West in order to maintain a grip on power. By C.J. Chivers.  With a graphic.  CIA-UNREST (Washington) -- Since the arrival of its new director, Porter J. Goss, what has been playing out within the CIA headquarters is no less than a clash of cultures, on a scale not seen there since the Carter administration, when Stansfield Turner, the retired admiral, brought a half-dozen Navy officers with him to the agency in 1977. By Douglas Jehl.  KY-NUKE (Washington) -- An electrical fire on Tuesday afternoon at a uranium enrichment plant in Paducah, Ky., blacked out most of the equipment used to purify the uranium at a late stage in the enrichment process, the plant manager said Wednesday. Without the ability to withdraw the finished product from the system, operators were forced to mix enriched uranium with more diluted uranium in order to avoid exceeding safety limits on enrichment. The plant is the only domestic source of uranium enrichment. By Matthew L. Wald  CHINA-RELIGION (Huaide, China) -- China's growing material wealth has eluded the countryside, home to two-thirds of its population. But there is a bull market in sects and cults competing for souls. That has alarmed the authorities, who seem uncertain whether the spread of religion or its systematic repression does more to turn peasants against Communist rule. The demise of Communist ideology has left a spiritual void, and it is being filled by religion. The country today has more church-going Protestants than Europe, according to several foreign estimates. Buddhism has become popular among the social elite. But it is the rural underclass that is most desperate for salvation. By Joseph Kahn.  With photo.  THANKSGIVING-IMMIG (Paterson, N.J.) -- Yaser Baker is cooking turkey with a Palestinian recipe, despite the objections of some of his fellow Arab residents of Paterson. Leticia Maravilla of Los Angeles cooked her first turkey soon after arriving from Mexico, before she even got her green card. Thanksgiving, which began as a party for immigrants, remains the most accessible American holiday for newcomers. It requires no specific religious or political allegiance. Even if an immigrant comes from a culture where whole roast turkey is never on the menu -- and that's almost everywhere else except North America -- most are willing to give it a try. By cooking turkey, said Lucy Long, a professor of popular culture at Bowling Green State University, even if you don't like it, you are part of something bigger. Of course, that translates to a nation of cross-cultural Thanksgivings, where sticky rice stuffing edges out cornbread and curry and gravy fight for dominance on overloaded plates. By Kim Severson.  With photos.  FLA-HOMELESS (Pensacola, Fla.) -- Kelly Terrell's family of four is staying in an 8-foot-wide trailer on a concrete lot, surrounded by highways, megastores and a place called Bingo Paradise. The walls are thin, the neighbors too close, the bathroom too small to turn around in. Yet they are among the lucky. At the end of a hurricane season that left much of Florida in ruins, thousands of families remain displaced, the numbers still growing by the day. Water-damaged homes, perhaps livable at first, have sprouted black mold that sends invisible spores through the air. Money for hotel rooms has run out, as has the patience of friends with pullout couches. Gov. Jeb Bush and Michael Brown, director of the Federal Emergency Management Agency, said last month that every hurricane victim who needed it would have free temporary housing by Thanksgiving, a goal that they have raced in recent weeks to fulfill. But about 2,500 families are now waiting for the small trailers or larger mobile homes that FEMA provides for up to 18 months. By Abby Goodnough.  With photo.
